<div id="review_40539507" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40539507" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1060503-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229065192p2/1060503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40539507?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/1060503-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Sarah by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1066869-jackie">Jackie</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1060503-sarah?shelf=2009">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40539507" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7959957224409979437">This book is absolutely unbelievable. It's no surprise it's considered one of the most important books of the 1960s. Erich Fromm's ideas about what "loving" means are mind-blowing, and bring to the forefront every problem I've witnessed in myself, and in others, in relationships.<br/>It's not a stupid self-help guide on how to love people or something cheesy like that. It's an honest look at how people's perceptions of love and loving have changed. And it's sensational.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2754742074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12221296958334142587"><b>“Love isn't something natural. Rather it requires discipline, concentration, patience, faith, and the overcoming of narcissism. It isn't a feeling, it is a practice.”</b><br/><br/>Is it a coincidence that I embark on the journey of reading Fromm’s Art of Loving right after I finished reading Eagleton’s Meaning of Life –which he concluded by considering ‘love’ as the ultimate answer for the question of existence? <br/><br/>To be honest, I don’t believe in coincidences. It must be destiny.<br/><br/>‎‫‏<img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1553268056ra/27251013.gif' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>‬‬‬‬‬‬‬‬<br/><br/>In this book, Fromm e</span>
  <span id="freeText12221296958334142587" style="display:none"><b>“Love isn't something natural. Rather it requires discipline, concentration, patience, faith, and the overcoming of narcissism. It isn't a feeling, it is a practice.”</b><br><br>Is it a coincidence that I embark on the journey of reading Fromm’s Art of Loving right after I finished reading Eagleton’s Meaning of Life –which he concluded by considering ‘love’ as the ultimate answer for the question of existence? <br><br>To be honest, I don’t believe in coincidences. It must be destiny.<br><br>‎‫‏<img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1553268056ra/27251013.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">‬‬‬‬‬‬‬‬<br><br>In this book, Fromm explored LOVE thoroughly beginning from ‘brotherly love’ and eventually ending with ‘love of God’- which he considered as the purest form of love there is.<br><br><br>The whole book was short and pleasant and reading it was truly interesting. I didn’t feel bored at all. <br><br>This is the fifth book I read for Erich Fromm (I guess) and I intend on reading the rest of his books. I get new ideas and perspectives from each book I read for him.</span>

<div id="review_1072673838" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1072673838" />
    <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid"><img alt="Farhan Khalid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350661583p2/2881911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1072673838?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Farhan Khalid" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid">Farhan Khalid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1072673838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11291174303048430947">The process of learning an art can be divided into two parts:<br/><br/>One, the mastery of the theory<br/><br/>The other, the mastery of the practice<br/><br/>The mastery of the art must be a matter of ultimate concern<br/><br/>Man is gifted with reason<br/><br/>He is life being aware of itself<br/><br/>He has awareness of himself, of his fellow man, of his past, and of the possibilities of his future<br/><br/>There is no good and evil unless there is freedom to disobey<br/><br/>After man and woman have become aware of themselves and of each other, they are aware of their s</span>
  <span id="freeText11291174303048430947" style="display:none">The process of learning an art can be divided into two parts:<br /><br />One, the mastery of the theory<br /><br />The other, the mastery of the practice<br /><br />The mastery of the art must be a matter of ultimate concern<br /><br />Man is gifted with reason<br /><br />He is life being aware of itself<br /><br />He has awareness of himself, of his fellow man, of his past, and of the possibilities of his future<br /><br />There is no good and evil unless there is freedom to disobey<br /><br />After man and woman have become aware of themselves and of each other, they are aware of their separateness<br /><br />Adam defends himself by blaming Eve, rather than trying to defend her<br /><br />The awareness of human separation, without reunion by love is the source of shame, guilty and anxiety<br /><br />The deepest need of man, then, is the need to overcome his separateness, to leave the prison of his aloneness<br /><br />The absolute failure to achieve this aim means insanity<br /><br />But the more the human race emerges from these primary bonds, the more it separate itself from the natural world, the more intense becomes the need to find new ways of escaping separateness<br /><br />It seems that after the orgiastic experience, man can go on for a time without suffering too much from his separateness<br /><br />The sexual act without love never bridges the gap between two human beings, except momentarily<br /><br />Equality today means "sameness" rather than "oneness"<br /><br />The desire for interpersonal fusion is the most powerful striving in man<br /><br />The failure to achieve it means insanity or destruction - self destruction or destruction of others<br /><br />There can be masochistic submission to fate, to sickness, to rhythmic music, to the orgiastic state produced by drugs or under hypnotic trance - in all these instances the person renounces his integrity, makes himself the instrument of somebody or something outside of himself<br /><br />The sadistic person commands, exploits, hurts, humiliates, and the masochistic person commanded, exploited, hurt, humiliated<br /><br />This is a difference in a realistic sense: fusion without integrity<br /><br />Mature love is union under the condition of pressing one's integrity, one's individuality<br /><br />In love the paradox occurs that two beings become one and yet remain two<br /><br />Spinoza: the virtue and power are one and same<br /><br />Envy, jealousy, ambition, any kind of greed are passions<br /><br />Love is an action, the practice of human power, which can be practiced only in freedom and never as the result of a compulsion<br /><br />Love is an activity, not a passive affect<br /><br />Love is a "standing" not a "falling for"<br /><br />The active character of love can be described by stating that love is primarily giving, not receiving<br /><br />Giving is more joyous than receiving, not because it is a deprivation, but because in the act of giving lies the expression of my aliveness<br /><br />The basic need to fuse with another person so as to transcend the prison of one's separateness is closely related to another specifically human desire, that to know the "secret of man":<br /><br />Way of complete power over another person<br /><br />The other path to knowing "the secret" is love<br /><br />Love is active penetration of the other person<br /><br />The problem of knowing man is parallel to the religious problem of knowing God<br /><br />The idea of polarization of masculine and feminine is most strikingly expressed in the myth that originally man and woman were one<br /><br />In the love between man and woman, each of them is reborn<br /><br />Infantile love: I am loved because I am <br /><br />Or: I love because I am loved<br /><br />Mature love: I am loved because I love<br /><br />Immature love: I love you because I need you<br /><br />Mature love: I need you because I love you<br /><br />The development of the capacity of love is the development of the object of love<br /><br />The relationship to mother loses some of its vital significance, and instead the relationship to father becomes more and more important<br /><br />Father does not represent the natural world<br /><br />He represents the other pole of human existence: the world of thought, of man-made things, of law and order, of discipline, of travel and adventure<br /><br />Father is the one who teaches the child, who shows him the road into the world<br /><br />Fatherly love is conditional love<br /><br />Its principle is "I love you because you fulfil my expectations ,because you do your duty, because you are like me"<br /><br />Obedience becomes the main virtue, disobedience is the main sin<br /><br />I can work for it, his love is not outside of my control as motherly love is<br /><br />If a person loves only one other person and is indifferent to the rest of his fellow men, his love is not love but a symbiotic attachment, or an enlarged egotism<br /><br />Brotherly love is based on the experience that we all are one<br /><br />Brotherly love is love between equals<br /><br />In erotic love, two people who were separate become one<br /><br />In motherly love, two people who were one become separate<br /><br />A woman can be a truly loving mother if she can love, if she is able to love her husband, other children, strangers, all human beings<br /><br />Erotic love is by its very nature exclusive and not universal<br /><br />It is perhaps the most deceptive form of love there is<br /><br />Sexual desire can be stimulated by the anxiety of aloneness, by the wish to conquer or conquered, by vanity, by the wish to hurt and even to destroy, as much as it can be stimulated by love<br /><br />Married couple: they are two people who identify themselves with each other, and who solve the problem of separateness by enlarging the single individual into two. They have the experience of overcoming aloneness<br /><br />If love were only a feeling, there would be no basis for the promise to love each other forever. A feeling comes and it may go<br /><br />Love is exclusively an act of will and commitment<br /><br />Love of others and love of ourselves are not alternatives <br /><br />Love, in principle, is indivisible as far as the connection between "objects" and one's own self is concerned <br /><br />Genuine love is an expression of productiveness and implies care, respect, responsibility and knowledge<br /><br />If an individual is able to love productively, he loves himself too, if he can love only others, he cannot love at all<br /><br />Meister Eckhart: if you love yourself, you love everybody else as you do yourself<br /><br />The understanding of concept of God must start with an analysis of character structure of the person who worships<br /><br />The concept of God is only a historical conditioned one in which man has expressed his experience of his higher powers, his longing for truth and unity<br /><br />The teacher of paradoxical logic says that man can perceive reality only in contradictions<br /><br />From the standpoint of paradoxical logic the emphasis is not on thought, but on the act<br /><br />Paradoxical thought led to tolerance and an effort toward self-transformation<br /><br />The Aristotelian standpoint led to dogma and science, to the Catholic Church, and to the discovery of atomic energy<br /><br />In Western religious system: the love of God is belief in God, in God's existence, God's justice, God's love<br /><br />In the Eastern religions and mysticism: the love of God is an intense feeling experience of oneness<br /><br />The love for God cannot be separated from the love for one's parents<br /><br />Modern capitalism needs men who cooperate smoothly and in large numbers, who want to consume more and more, whose tastes are standardized and can be influenced and anticipated<br /><br />Modern man is alienated from himself, from his fellow men, and from nature<br /><br />The strict routine of bureaucratized, mechanical work, which helps people to remain unaware of their most fundamental human desires, of the longing for transcendence and unity<br /><br />Amusement industry <br /><br />Idea of the team<br /><br />Love is not the result of adequate sexual satisfaction, but sexual happiness<br /><br />For Freud love is in itself an irrational phenomenon<br /><br />Falling in love always verges on the abnormal, is always accompanied by blindness to reality and compulsiveness<br /><br />Love as mutual sexual satisfaction and love as "teamwork" and as haven from aloneness, are two "normal" forms of the disintegration of love in modern Western society<br /><br />The basic condition for neurotic love lies in the fact that one or both of the "lovers" have remained attached to the figure of a parent<br /><br />These men still feel like children; they want mother's protection, love, warmth, care, and admiration<br /><br />Their aim is to be loved, not to love<br /><br />If they have found the right women, they feel secure and on the top of the world<br /><br />If failed: The man feels deeply hurt and disappointed<br /><br />Idolatrous love: man loses himself in the loved person instead of finding himself<br /><br /><br />Sentimental love: love is experienced only in the phantasy<br /><br />Consumers of screen pictures, magazine love stories and love songs<br /><br />Projective mechanisms:<br /><br />	1. Avoiding own problems, and being concerned with the defects of "loved" person<br /><br />	2. Projection of one's own problem on the children<br /><br />Love necessarily means the absence of conflict<br /><br />Love is constant challenge. It is not a resting place but a moving, growing, working together<br /><br />Life has no goal except the one to move, no principle except the one of fair exchange, no satisfaction except the one to consume<br /><br />The belief in God has been transformed into a psychological device to make one better fitted for the competitive struggle<br /><br />The practice of an art requires discipline, concentration, patience and supreme concern<br /><br />Without discipline, life becomes shattered, chaotic, and lacks in concentration<br /><br />One does not begin to learn an art directly, but indirectly<br /><br />Concentration is to learn to be alone with oneself without doing anything<br /><br />Concentration means to be able to be alone with oneself<br /><br />	1. Try to see white screen<br /><br />	2. Follow one's breathing<br /><br />	3. I = myself, as the center of my powers, as the creator of my world<br /><br />One must learn to be concentrated in everything one does<br /><br />To be concentrated in relation to others means primarily to be able to listen<br /><br />One cannot learn to concentrate without becoming sensitive to oneself<br /><br />Analyze oneself<br /><br />State of relaxed alertness<br /><br />The opposite pole to narcissism is objectivity <br /><br />The faculty to think objectively is reason<br /><br />Emotional attitude behind reason is that of humility<br /><br />The ability to love depends on one's capacity to emerge from narcissism<br /><br />The practice of the art of loving requires the practice of faith<br /><br />Rational love: certainty and firmness<br /><br />Irrational love: one's submission to irrational authority<br /><br />Rational love is rooted in productive intellectual and emotional activity<br /><br />In experience, confidence, power of thought, observation and judgment<br /><br />Education is identical with helping the child realize his potentialities<br /><br />The basis of rational faith is productiveness<br /><br />To have faith requires courage, the ability to take a risk, the readiness to accept pain and disappointment<br /><br />Whoever insists on safety and security as primary conditions of life cannot have faith<br /><br />The faith in power is opposite of the faith in love<br /><br />Love is an act of faith<br /><br />The capacity to love demands a state of intensity, awakeness and enhanced vitality<br /><br />Love is the only sane and satisfactory answer to the problem of human existence</span>
  <a data-text-id="11291174303048430947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Hoda Marmar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3655700-hoda-marmar"><img alt="Hoda Marmar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572216889p2/3655700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388665449?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hoda Marmar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hoda Marmar" href="/user/show/3655700-hoda-marmar">Hoda Marmar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=bookoholics">bookoholics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=12genres">12genres</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655700-hoda-marmar?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388665449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14351509685486012892">The Art of Loving by Erich Fromm was first published in 1956. It addresses love as an art to practice and master, rather than as a fleeting emotional mystery or a magical lightning strike. He argues that love is holistic and should not be seen as separate from man's nature. Love inherently connects all aspects of man, and all forms of love (brotherly, erotic, parent-child, God, motherly, fatherly, and self loves) are the manifestation of one's way of living (loving or non-loving), his tolerance,</span>
  <span id="freeText14351509685486012892" style="display:none">The Art of Loving by Erich Fromm was first published in 1956. It addresses love as an art to practice and master, rather than as a fleeting emotional mystery or a magical lightning strike. He argues that love is holistic and should not be seen as separate from man's nature. Love inherently connects all aspects of man, and all forms of love (brotherly, erotic, parent-child, God, motherly, fatherly, and self loves) are the manifestation of one's way of living (loving or non-loving), his tolerance, and his ability to grow. The transforming man seeks mature love and faces his alienation in a capitalist society and his anxiety of being separated from others through discipline, concentration, patience, and practice in all aspects of his life. He learns to cope with being truly alone, and seeks only meaningful bonds and behaviors. Love is a way of living, and not just an emotion or an instinct.<br /><br />Erich Fromm's writing style makes it easy for the non-academic reader to follow through. His ideas are well organized into chapters, and each chapter into subtitles, and within each subtitle, many paragraphs are interlinked and flow smoothly and logically. He gives an explanation or an example when needed. He revisits a concept to further develop it when it helps his argument. He shows pros and cons, and opposing schools of thought before making his own statement such as Aritotle's logic versus Taoist paradoxical logic, Sullivan versus Freud on defining love, and Eastern versus Western spirituality. When he talks of neurotic and projection-al loves, he gives extensive examples of love self-concept gone wrong and unhealthy fixation to a parent figure in the course of its development. In short, the organization and fluidity of the information is so neat and easy to follow. Kudos to Erich!<br /><br />In his arguments, Fromm contrasts many notions, some of which are the following:<br />•	Art vs. chance<br />•	Being loved vs. loving<br />•	Object of love vs. its function<br />•	Falling in love vs. being in love<br />•	Motherly unconditional love vs. fatherly conditional love<br />•	Self love: selfishness vs. unselfishness<br />•	Education vs. manipulation<br />•	Love thy neighbor vs. radical nihilism in a capitalist society<br /> <br />The whole book falls into 4 chapters and 133 pages. It is highly quotable and quite insightful about how love develops psychologically and socially in its many forms. Reading it leads to a better understanding of why we do what we do, why we have certain beliefs and confusions about love and ourselves, and what might hinder our personal growth and our ability to love. Fromm argues that only mature love, stemming from care and responsibility for one another's growth (among other characteristics), only that genuine love can help us face our mortality and the injustice of life and death. <br /><br />I highly recommend this book to those who are willing to introspect and dig deeper into the depths of their psyche and face their insecurities and misconceptions about love, because I firmly believe that nobody fully knows how to love oneself, others, and the world. This book is a good building block; it taught me plenty. I will sum up the book with a line from Neruda:<br />“If nothing saves us from death, at least love should save us from life.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="14351509685486012892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1951048794" />
    <a title="Philipp" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8325737-philipp"><img alt="Philipp" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392618768p2/8325737.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1951048794?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philipp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philipp" href="/user/show/8325737-philipp">Philipp</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8325737-philipp?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8325737-philipp?shelf=psychoanalysis">psychoanalysis</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8325737-philipp?shelf=on-living">on-living</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1951048794" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6874466508721148786">The first time I read this book I was doing my undergrad - about ten years ago, given to me by a girlfriend at that time. I should've read this closer.<br/><br/>Anyway, the central thesis is that love is something very different from what popular/current culture tells you it is. It's not infatuation with a person (even thought his definition encompasses this), it's an attitude, the way a person relates to the world. It's a far cry from the weird American cover which makes it look like a self-help book, it</span>
  <span id="freeText6874466508721148786" style="display:none">The first time I read this book I was doing my undergrad - about ten years ago, given to me by a girlfriend at that time. I should've read this closer.<br /><br />Anyway, the central thesis is that love is something very different from what popular/current culture tells you it is. It's not infatuation with a person (even thought his definition encompasses this), it's an attitude, the way a person relates to the world. It's a far cry from the weird American cover which makes it look like a self-help book, it's not really that - it's interpretative psychoanalysis in same vein as Freud, very little data, lots of art (a similar and also great book is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2761.The_Denial_of_Death" title="The Denial of Death by Ernest Becker" rel="nofollow noopener">The Denial of Death</a>). Some things I would like to stuff into a time machine to younger me ('Most people see the problem of love primarily as that of being loved, rather than that of loving, of one's capacity to love. Hence the problem to them is how to be loved, how to be lovable.'), some other stuff is clearly a product of the 50s (to him, homosexuals are incapable of true love - transsexuals don't even exist - gender roles are pretty strict, with maternal love (a caring love) and paternal love (a teaching and directing love) being different and being only available to their respective gender). <br /><br />As it was popular back then it's closer to philosophy than to psychology - he often uses Freud's teachings about love and sexuality as starting points for his own philosophy on love. I wouldn't say it's required, but it's definitely recommended reading - some points fall too short and are underdiscussed, I'm hoping <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67977.The_Sane_Society" title="The Sane Society by Erich Fromm" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sane Society</a> will be more in-depth.<br /><br />P.S.: Bonus quote:<br /><br /><blockquote><br />I should add here that just as it is important to avoid trivial conversation, it is important to avoid bad company. By bad company I do not refer only to people who are vicious and destructive; one should avoid their company because their orbit is poisonous and depressing. I mean also the company of zombies, of people whose soul is dead, although their body is alive; of people whose thoughts and conversation are trivial; who chatter instead of talk, and who assert cliche opinions instead of thinking.<br /></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="6874466508721148786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Hans" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1142366-hans"><img alt="Hans" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292037372p2/1142366.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75093874?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hans" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hans" href="/user/show/1142366-hans">Hans</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1142366-hans?shelf=spiritual-development">spiritual-development</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1142366-hans?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75093874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4498035086957410926">I would love to have been one Erich Fromm's close friends because we would have gotten along smashingly. Luckily for me he chose to be an author so I could at least get to know him through his immortal writings. <br/><br/>This was yet another fantastic and well written book. My favorite concept that really struck me was his explanation of how American Culture though materially wealthy leaves most of us with strong feeling of alienation from others. Fromm explains that this alienation is, in part, due to o</span>
  <span id="freeText4498035086957410926" style="display:none">I would love to have been one Erich Fromm's close friends because we would have gotten along smashingly. Luckily for me he chose to be an author so I could at least get to know him through his immortal writings. <br /><br />This was yet another fantastic and well written book. My favorite concept that really struck me was his explanation of how American Culture though materially wealthy leaves most of us with strong feeling of alienation from others. Fromm explains that this alienation is, in part, due to our obsession with wanting to be loved rather than developing the capacity to love. We work hard on our appearance, our charm, our sex appeal to try and 'win' love. When our focus instead should be on learning how to grow our hearts so we can love ourselves and others. He believes this can only be achieved like the mastery of any art, through self-discipline, focus, and concentrated effort. Enduring Love is active, and our concept of "falling in love" is passive.<br /><br />Here are my favorite excerpts <br />"Love is the only sane and satisfactory answer to the problem of human existence, then any society which excludes, relatively, the development of love, must in the long run perish of its own contradiction with the basic necessities of human nature"<br /><br />"Human values have become determined by economic values. What is good for machines must be good for man --so goes the logic. Modern man thinks he loses something-time--when he does not do things quickly; yet he does not know what to do with the time he gains --except kill it"<br /> <br />"With regard to to the the art of loving, this means that anyone who aspire to become a master in this art must begin by practicing discipline, concentration and patience throughout every phase of his life"<br /> <br />"Irrational faith is the acceptance of something as true only because an authority or the majority say so, rational faith is rooted in an independent conviction based upon one's own productive observing and thinking, in spite of the majority's opinion"<br /> <br />"The root of the word education is e-ducere, literally, to lead forth, or to bring out something which is potentially present"<br /><br />"Love is possible only if two persons communicate with each other from the center of their existence, hence if each one of them experiences himself from the center of his existence. Only in this 'central experience" is human reality, only here is aliveness, only here is the basis for love. Love, experienced thus is a constant challenge; it is not a resting place, but a moving, growing, working together...they are one with each other by being one with themselves"<br /> <br />"First the practice of an art requires discipline. I shall never be good at anything if I do only if "I am in the mood" may be a nice or amusing hobby, but I shall never become a master in that art...Without such discipline, however, life becomes shattered, chaotic, and lacks in concentration".<br /> <br />"If his mother, in fact 'loved' him in an overprotective manner, if he finds a wife of the same motherly type, if his special gifts and talents permit him to use his charm and be admired, he is 'well adjusted' in a social sence, without ever reaching a higher level of maturity. But under less favorable conditions and these are naturally more frequent-his love life, if not his social life, will be a serious disappointment; conflicts, and frequently intense anxiety and depression arise when this type of personality is left alone"<br /> <br />"This idolatrous love is often described as the true, great love; but while it is meant to portray the intensity and depth of love, it only demonstrates the hunger and despair of the idolator".<br /><br /><br />"The world of thought remains caught in the paradox. Th only way in which the world can be grasped ultimately lies, not in thought, but in the act, in the experience of oneness. Thus paradoxical logic leads to the conclusion that the love of God is neither the knowledge of God in thought, nor the thought of one's love of God, but the act of experiencing the oneness with God"<br /><br />"One thing is certain: the nature of his love for God corresponds to the nature of his love for man, and furthermore, the real quality of his love for God and man often is unconscious--covered up and rationalized by a more mature thought of what his love is."<br /><br />"The economic structure is reflected in a hierarchy of values. Capital commans labor; amassed things, that which is dead, are of superior value to labor, to human powers, to that which is alive"<br /><br />"Modern capitalism needs men who cooperate smoothly and in large numbers; who want to consume more and more; and whose tastes are standardized and can be easily influenced and anticipated"<br /><br />"Human relations are essentially those of alienated automatons, each basing his security on staying close to the herd, and not being different in thought, feeling or action. While everybody tries to be as close as possible to the rest, everyody remains utterly alone, pervaded by the deep sense of insecurity, anxiety and guilt which always results when huamn separateness cannot be overcome"<br /><br />"Mature love is union under the condition of preserving one's integrity, one's individuality"; a power which breaks through the walls which separate man from his fellow men, which unites him with others; love makes him overcome the sense of isolation and separateness, yet it permits him to be himself, to retain his integrity"<br /><br />"Almost half of marriage end in divorce, in part because of the temptation to "trade-up" as if each individual has a fixed worth, some calculable integration of youth, beauty, cleverness, status and (especially) wealth. Often as not these rearrangements result in marriages that feel empty"<br /> <br />"even love as a haven from aloneness is bound to fail"<br /> <br />"Most people see the problem of love primarily as that of being loved, rather than that of loving, of one's capacity to love"<br /> <br />"'Attractive' usually means a nice package of qualities that are popular and sought after on the personality market".<br /> <br />Man, in an attempt to overcome his sense of separateness, uses conformity to seek union'</span>
  <a data-text-id="4498035086957410926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19176667" />
    <a title="Erik Graff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff"><img alt="Erik Graff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299888977p2/974210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19176667?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik Graff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik Graff" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff">Erik Graff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/5559501-the-art-of-loving">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19176667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17097099932241286874">Although I'd avoided most of the "popular" books in high school (The History of Torture being the best seller in the Maine Township H.S. South bookstore), I made an exception for this one because I had heard that Fromm was some kind of socialist--and, heck, I was agonizing a lot about one kind of troublous love in those days. I'm glad to have made the exception because this was useful introduction to the complexity of language, in this case as regards the use of the English word "love". Furtherm</span>
  <span id="freeText17097099932241286874" style="display:none">Although I'd avoided most of the "popular" books in high school (The History of Torture being the best seller in the Maine Township H.S. South bookstore), I made an exception for this one because I had heard that Fromm was some kind of socialist--and, heck, I was agonizing a lot about one kind of troublous love in those days. I'm glad to have made the exception because this was useful introduction to the complexity of language, in this case as regards the use of the English word "love". Furthermore, it introduced me to the idea that other languages, Greek in this case, can offer a greater variety of nuance than our own. Having received from Dr. Fromm a greater precision in language allowed a greater precision of thinking that helped me survive adolescence.<br /><br />I read this edition on the patio of the Malmstadt's summer house overlooking Lake Michigan while they were out of town. They had the more comfortable patio furniture, the best view and, besides, I could get away from my demanding little brother, then nine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17097099932241286874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1357631857" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1357631857" />
    <a title="mis fit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2394223-mis-fit"><img alt="mis fit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1426547063p2/2394223.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1357631857?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mis fit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mis fit" href="/user/show/2394223-mis-fit">mis fit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2394223-mis-fit?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2394223-mis-fit?shelf=social-psychology">social-psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2394223-mis-fit?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1357631857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12463957103532438214">I read this book a couple months back, but I’ve been letting it simmer and slowly collecting my thoughts. I didn’t want to just be done with it-- I wanted to grapple with Fromm’s arguments in the context of my everyday experience. His opinions often seem convincing at first glance, but to what extent do they really hold up? His work has it’s faults (like a bunch of weird homophobic statements in this one…), but I’ve been intrigued by Fromm’s social psychology since I first encountered <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67977.The_Sane_Society' title='The Sane Society by Erich Fromm' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sane S</a></span>
  <span id="freeText12463957103532438214" style="display:none">I read this book a couple months back, but I’ve been letting it simmer and slowly collecting my thoughts. I didn’t want to just be done with it-- I wanted to grapple with Fromm’s arguments in the context of my everyday experience. His opinions often seem convincing at first glance, but to what extent do they really hold up? His work has it’s faults (like a bunch of weird homophobic statements in this one…), but I’ve been intrigued by Fromm’s social psychology since I first encountered <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67977.The_Sane_Society" title="The Sane Society by Erich Fromm" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sane Society</a> a few years ago.<br /><br />Fromm argues that popular, romantic conceptions of love are often very flawed. He critiques the widespread notion that love is an emotion to be felt or an end state to arrive at. For Fromm, love is a creative capacity. It’s built on choices and actions we perform everyday. It is behavior that is characterized by care, responsibility, respect, and knowledge. We don’t just find “the right person” and magically end up in a state of love-- we must learn how to love.<br /><br />Fromm points out the dangers of conceptualizing love as something to “fall” into. It takes away the agency we all have to be loving and obscures what loving behavior is comprised of. It makes love into a thing that one either possesses or lacks, an emotion that must be acquired by way of popularity-- by having the right personality, clothes, and lifestyle that is attractive to an equally popular mate. What I like best about this critique is Fromm’s attention to the social context. He was, of course, a sociologist and that background allowed him to pay special attention to the influence of social structure and economic relations in individual psychology. Fromm’s critique of love in Western society shows that our most private emotions and intimate relationships are profoundly shaped by modern consumer society. The larger forces that we tend to think of as structural do in fact shape how we see ourselves, others, and how we go about loving one another. This is such an important point-- one that has been developed further by sociologists like Arlie Russell Hochschild (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/268673.The_Managed_Heart_Commercialization_of_Human_Feeling" title="The Managed Heart Commercialization of Human Feeling by Arlie Russell Hochschild" rel="nofollow noopener">The Managed Heart: Commercialization of Human Feeling</a>), Miliann Kang (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8870813.The_Managed_Hand_Race__Gender__and_the_Body_in_Beauty_Service_Work" title="The Managed Hand Race, Gender, and the Body in Beauty Service Work by Miliann Kang" rel="nofollow noopener">The Managed Hand: Race, Gender, and the Body in Beauty Service Work</a>), and Eva Illouz (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/339859.Consuming_the_Romantic_Utopia_Love_and_the_Cultural_Contradictions_of_Capitalism" title="Consuming the Romantic Utopia Love and the Cultural Contradictions of Capitalism by Eva Illouz" rel="nofollow noopener">Consuming the Romantic Utopia: Love and the Cultural Contradictions of Capitalism</a>), to name a few.<br /><br />Fromm also argues that one must be an independent, whole person in order to love well, to love in a healthy way. He slogs through a bunch of Freudian psychology, making some good points and some not so good points, in a section on love between parent and child. What’s valuable here though is that love can sometimes go awry. Considering the ideal types of motherly and fatherly love for child, we can see how a child often starts off completely connected, undifferentiated from the mother figure. As the child grows, the mother’s unconditional love is contrasted by the father’s conditional love-- to receive love from the father, the child must live up to the father’s expectations and wants, they must be good enough. And if they are, only then do they receive father’s love. These motherly and fatherly types of love are limiting when they recur in romantic relationships between adults. To put it simply, we can’t be children if we want to learn the art of loving. Only independent adults can master the skills of care, responsibility, respect, and knowledge. This is important and radical-- only through independence can we come into contact with each other and bridge that often painful gap between ourselves and others, addressing a very human longing for connection.<br /><br />Overall, love is a very social phenomenon. It is embedded in the way society is structured, how people are raised, and what options are available for fulfilling the need for human connection. This is an important book, and many of these arguments will stick with me I’m sure.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12463957103532438214" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1453146482" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1453146482" />
    <a title="Pankaj Suneja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20857603-pankaj-suneja"><img alt="Pankaj Suneja" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1453146482?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pankaj Suneja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pankaj Suneja" href="/user/show/20857603-pankaj-suneja">Pankaj Suneja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780826412607" href="/book/show/3191907-the-art-of-loving">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1453146482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8819974947385829914">"The Art of Loving" is excellent critical commentary of society driven by economic and commodity values. In this context, the meaning of love is lost which Erich attempts to reinvent. <br/>Erich writes that love is an art. And so it requires effort and knowledge.<br/>Most of us are occupied with finding the right person, or the object of love, and not concerned about the faculty of function of love. <br/>The book is divided in three main chapters. In first, he put forths the theory of love, second chapter incl</span>
  <span id="freeText8819974947385829914" style="display:none">"The Art of Loving" is excellent critical commentary of society driven by economic and commodity values. In this context, the meaning of love is lost which Erich attempts to reinvent. <br />Erich writes that love is an art. And so it requires effort and knowledge.<br />Most of us are occupied with finding the right person, or the object of love, and not concerned about the faculty of function of love. <br />The book is divided in three main chapters. In first, he put forths the theory of love, second chapter includes disintegration of love in present western society. He does seem to value to eastern society, but i feel the idea of love that is existing (i need to be loved) is universal. And the third chapter includes practice of love.<br />I am someone who has not read a lot on love. I do not remember about Greek philosophers like Socrates, and indian religious or philosopher talks about love.<br />After reading this book, I feel love is difficult. And the same feeling I got when I read Rainer Maria Rilke's view on love. He said love is difficult, and isn't it true that just because it is difficult we must aspire toward it. I am still contemplating why and how we should be motivated to achieve something difficult. Most people are content with immediate pleasure. Definitely, it feels more real, closer to truth, genuine to go through the difficult experience, but it cannot b forced or pushed. <br />It is essential that human beings are alone. we are separate. Now this separateness is difficult to deal with, so Erich formulates three kind of union. The sexual union which is transitory experience. The union during creative process which is not interpersonal. The third form of union is love. Love is of two kinds. the symbiotic love, which sadistic-masochistic in nature, and which is immature and it means falling in love. But the second form of love, that Erich writes about is one in which person stands in love. <br />He writes about different kinds of love, brotherly love, mother love, father love, sister love, erotic love and love of god. I agree that father love is conditional while mother's love is unconditional.<br />In practice of love, Erich writes about important certain qualities like feeling of humility, concentration, patience, discipline as important to have capacity to give and love. It is very important to be comfortable with being alone, and then only a person can love. Rilke too write about importance of being alone. let the sadness come inside you and transform you. <br />These qualities are difficult to achieve, and rightly so Erich points out at the incompatibility of the modern, practical, city life and the life dedicated to higher order of being.<br />I liked many thoughts and ideas in the book, that I think could bring change in perspective. One reason I like this book is that it talks about divided worlds that very few people are able to perceive. The world of practical matters and the other world focuses toward soulful and spiritual practice. Erich writes love as soulful experience, and hence how the notion of love is disintegrated in practical world. <br />Erich is psychoanalyst, and he does detail discussion of Freud's understanding of love, which I was only partially interested in. What I got is that Freud never developed idea on love. His thought remained till sexuality.<br />This book reverberates in me because I had been preoccupied with thought that for how long I could receive and could not give. This book talks about giving and the aliveness that comes from giving. And surely it is an art and it needs to be practiced.<br />Lastly I would want to write this quote from the book, that left a mark on me.<br />"He is a good father but at the same time authoritarian. Whenever he is pleased with the son's conduct he praises him, gives him presents is affectionate; whenever the son displeases him, he withdraws, or scolds,. The son for whom father's affection is the only one he has, becomes attached to father in a slavish way. His main aim in life is to please father- and when he succeeds he feels happy, secure and satisfied. But when he makes a mistake, fails of does not succeed in pleasing, he feels deflated, unloved, cast out" (Pg 88)<br />I think it is very strong and meaningful writing. <br />It is wonderful book. One can imagine how much aloneness must one one have experienced in writing the book. And hence he could give us the book. Something valuable. It is life worth living, I ask. Life lived with penetration, and search, and genuineness is worth living. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8819974947385829914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_79763441" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/79763441" />
    <a title="Mir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/237469-mir"><img alt="Mir" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275489063p2/237469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79763441?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mir" href="/user/show/237469-mir">Mir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/237469-mir?shelf=ideas">ideas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79763441" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16765656907033249639">My edition of this work (New York: Harper & Brothers, 1956) was part of the World Perspectives series, which "endeavors to show that the conception of wholeness, unity, organism is a higher and more concrete conception than that of matter and energy... For the principle of life consists in the tension which connects the spirit with the realm of matter" and that "Knowledge, as it is shown in these books, no longer consists in a manipulation of man and nature as opposite forces, nor in the reducti</span>
  <span id="freeText16765656907033249639" style="display:none">My edition of this work (New York: Harper &amp; Brothers, 1956) was part of the World Perspectives series, which "endeavors to show that the conception of wholeness, unity, organism is a higher and more concrete conception than that of matter and energy... For the principle of life consists in the tension which connects the spirit with the realm of matter" and that "Knowledge, as it is shown in these books, no longer consists in a manipulation of man and nature as opposite forces, nor in the reduction of data to mere statistical order, but is a means of liberating mankind from the destructive power of fear" (Intro by Ruth Nanda Anshen, x).<br /><br />Fromm begins, “Is love an art? Then it requires knowledge and effort." He argues that love is not, as many people erroneously believe, "a pleasant sensation, which to experience is a matter of chance, something one 'falls into' if one is lucky." People misunderstand the nature of love because they are overly focused on being loved rather than loving. Therefore they focus on things which will make them more attractive, such as status, money, or appearance; in short, “What most people in our culture mean by being lovable is essentially a mixture between being popular and having sex appeal.” He blames this partly on contemporary commodity culture, in which people look at one another as objects to buy or win: "For the man the attractive girl--and for the woman the attractive man--are prizes they are after. 'Attractive' usually means a nice package of qualities which are popular and sought after."<br /><br />"Love is an activity, not a passive affect." (22)<br /><br />Fromm on the story of Adam and Eve: "Should we assume that a myth as old and elementary as this has the prudish morals of the nineteenth-century outlook, and that the important point that the story wants to convey to us the embarrassment that their genitals were visible? This can hardly be so, and by understanding the story in a Victorian spirit, we miss the main point... after man and woman have become aware of themselves and of each other, they are aware of their separateness, and of their difference... But while recognizing their separateness they remain strangers, because they have not yet learned to love each other (as is also made very clear by the fact that Adam defends himself by blaming Eve).</span>
  <a data-text-id="16765656907033249639" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_332629254" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/332629254" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2763719-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564310959p2/2763719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/332629254?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/2763719-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/5559501-the-art-of-loving">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2763719-john?shelf=social-studies">social-studies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2763719-john?shelf=dnf">dnf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer332629254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10751473959283280423">The Art of Loving kicks off with some very valuable insights regarding love in modern Western culture. Fromm points out that we have altogether stopped viewing love as an act of will, but instead now see it as something that just effortlessly happens once we stumble upon the right person. He also ruminates on why relationships now tend to resemble business arrangements, rather than reflecting the traditional, self-sacrificing notion of marriage. When it comes to demonstrating the folly of such "</span>
  <span id="freeText10751473959283280423" style="display:none">The Art of Loving kicks off with some very valuable insights regarding love in modern Western culture. Fromm points out that we have altogether stopped viewing love as an act of will, but instead now see it as something that just effortlessly happens once we stumble upon the right person. He also ruminates on why relationships now tend to resemble business arrangements, rather than reflecting the traditional, self-sacrificing notion of marriage. When it comes to demonstrating the folly of such "forward thinking," Fromm seems right on the money; however, the book ultimately bogs itself down in dull prose that often uses psychobabble to disguise common sense as deep psychoanalytic insight. It's a book that is old-fashioned enough to frequently trot out the Bible to illustrate an effective point, but modern enough to regard such holy texts as being useful in a poetic sense only. Fromm happily paves over the pseudo-intellectual nonsense of Freud, but I don't particularly enjoy seeing him prop up Marx in Freud's place. Like most famous psychologists, Fromm too eagerly tries to force complex ideas into tiny little boxes in order to make them seem a lot less messy than they really are. Not a bad book per se, but one with a lot less practical application than is indicated by its title. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10751473959283280423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iG4e6blTRmSUiHa/9tB+yoQQR9CinBVMK9yWbgGeCSjIc3jSE8TC5x7ZKeMO2uxb4c0EjRvA+eASdxGhCjNf3w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

More than five million copies later, Dr. Eric Berne’s classic is as astonishing–and revealing–as it was on the day it was first published. This anniversary edition features a new introduction by Dr. James R. Allen, president of the International Transactional Analysis Association, and Kurt Vonnegut’s brilliant Life magazine review from 1965.\nWe play games all the time–sexual games, marital games, power games with our bosses, and competitive games with our friends. Detailing status contests like “Martini” (I know a better way), to lethal couples combat like “If It Weren’t For You” and “Uproar,” to flirtation favorites like “The Stocking Game” and “Let’s You and Him Fight,” Dr. Berne exposes the secret ploys and unconscious maneuvers that rule our intimate lives.\nExplosive when it first appeared, Games People Play is now widely recognized as the most original and influential popular psychology book of our time. It is both witness and tribute to the late theory of mind—the so-called structural theory, with its stress on aggression, indeed the death drive, as the pitiless adversary of eros.Civilization and Its Discontents is one of the last of Freud\'s books, written in the decade before his death and first published in German in 1929. In it he states his views on the broad question of man\'s place in the world, a place Freud defines in terms of ceaseless conflict between the individual\'s quest for freedom and society\'s demand for conformity.\nFreud\'s theme is that what works for civilization doesn\'t necessarily work for man. Man, by nature aggressive and egotistical, seeks self-satisfaction. But culture inhibits his instinctual drives. The result is a pervasive and familiar guilt.\nOf the various English translations of Freud\'s major works to appear in his lifetime, only one was authorized by Freud himself: The Standard Edition of the Complete Psychological Works of Sigmund Freud under the general editorship of James Strachey.\nFreud approved the overall editorial plan, specific renderings of key words and phrases, and the addition of valuable notes, from bibliographical and explanatory. Many of the translations were done by Strachey himself; the rest were prepared under his supervision. The result was to place the Standard Edition in a position of unquestioned supremacy over all other existing versions.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"18194451194879203356\" href=\"#\" Within a week, the study was abandoned, as ordinary college students were transformed into either brutal, sadistic guards or emotionally broken prisoners. \n\nBy illuminating the psychological causes behind such disturbing metamorphoses, Zimbardo enables us to better understand a variety of harrowing phenomena, from corporate malfeasance to organized genocide to how once upstanding American soldiers came to abuse and torture Iraqi detainees in Abu Ghraib. He replaces the long-held notion of the “bad apple” with that of the “bad barrel”—the idea that the social setting and the system contaminate the individual, rather than the other way His agenda in this tome, which was first published in 1978 but didn\'t become a bestseller until 1983, is to reconcile the psychoanalytic tradition with the conflicting cultural currents roiling the 70s. In the spirit of Me-Decade individualism and libertinism, he celebrates self-actualization as life\'s highest purpose and flirts with the notions of open marriage and therapeutic sex between patient and analyst. But because he is attuned to the nascent conservative backlash against the therapeutic worldview, Peck also cites Gospel passages, recruits psychotherapy to the cause of traditional religion (he even convinces a patient to sign up for divinity school) and insists that problems must be overcome through suffering, discipline and hard work (with a therapist.) Often departing from the cerebral and rationalistic bent of Freudian discourse for a mystical, Jungian tone more compatible with New Age spirituality, Peck writes of psychotherapy as an exercise in &quot;love&quot; and &quot;spiritual growth,&quot; asserts that &quot;our unconscious is God&quot; and affirms his belief in miracles, reincarnation and telepathy. Peck\'s synthesis of such clashing elements (he even throws in a little thermodynamics) is held together by a warm and lucid discussion of psychiatric principles and moving accounts of his own patients\' struggles and breakthroughs. Harmonizing psychoanalysis and spirituality, Christ and Buddha, Calvinist work ethic and interminable talking cures, this book is a touchstone of our contemporary religio-therapeutic culture.&quot; -- System 1 is fast, intuitive, and emotional; System 2 is slower, more deliberative, and more logical. Kahneman exposes the extraordinary capabilities—and also the faults and biases—of fast thinking, and reveals the pervasive influence of intuitive impressions on our thoughts and behavior. The impact of loss aversion and overconfidence on corporate strategies, the difficulties of predicting what will make us happy in the future, the challenges of properly framing risks at work and at home, the profound effect of cognitive biases on everything from playing the stock market to planning the next vacation—each of these can be understood only by knowing how the two systems work together to shape our judgments and decisions.\n\nEngaging the reader in a lively conversation about how we think, Kahneman reveals where we can and cannot trust our intuitions and how we can tap into the benefits of slow thinking. He offers practical and enlightening insights into how choices are made in both our business and our personal lives—and how we can use different techniques to guard against the mental glitches that often get us into trouble.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8788.Erich_Fromm">About Erich Fromm</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8788.Erich_Fromm"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1221544425p3/8788.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8788.Erich_Fromm">Erich Fromm
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,432
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12761007083882784024"><b>Erich Fromm,</b> Ph.D. (Sociology, University of Heidelberg, 1922), was a psychoanalyst and social philosopher who explored the interaction between psychology and society, and held various professorships in psychology in the U.S. and Mexico in the mid-20th century.<br/><br/>Fromm's theory is a rather unique blend of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10017.Freud' title='Freud' rel='nofollow noopener'>Freud</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7084.Marx' title='Marx' rel='nofollow noopener'>Marx</a>. Freud, of course, emphasized the unconscious, biological drives, repression, and</span>
  <span id="freeText12761007083882784024" style="display:none"><b>Erich Fromm,</b> Ph.D. (Sociology, University of Heidelberg, 1922), was a psychoanalyst and social philosopher who explored the interaction between psychology and society, and held various professorships in psychology in the U.S. and Mexico in the mid-20th century.<br /><br />Fromm's theory is a rather unique blend of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10017.Freud" title="Freud" rel="nofollow noopener">Freud</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7084.Marx" title="Marx" rel="nofollow noopener">Marx</a>. Freud, of course, emphasized the unconscious, biological drives, repression, and so on. In other words, Freud postulated that our characters were determined by biology. Marx, on the other hand, saw people as determined by their society, and most especially by their economic systems.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12761007083882784024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
